            }
            return "blank";
        }

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="d9084558e4964ce49129c6b579ce80e5">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '30232656-f088-4808-8b9e-35e9881ae3ea'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d32609339" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Hard carbon (HC) has become the most promising anode material for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs), but its plateau capacity at ≈0.1&nbsp;V (Na<sup>+</sup>/Na) is still much lower than that of graphite (372&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) in lithium-ion batteries (LIBs). Herein, a CO<sub>2</sub>-etching strategy is applied to generate abundant closed pores in starch-derived hard carbon that effectively enhances Na<sup>+</sup> plateau storage. During CO<sub>2</sub> etching, open pores are first formed on the carbon matrix, which are in situ reorganized to closed pores through high-temperature carbonization. This CO<sub>2</sub>-assisted pore-regulation strategy increases the diameter and the capacity of closed pores in HC, and simultaneously maintains the microsphere morphology (10–30&nbsp;µm in diameter). The optimal HC anode exhibits a Na-storage capacity of 487.6&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> with a high initial Coulomb efficiency of 90.56%. A record-high plateau capacity of 351&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> is achieved, owing to the abundant closed micropores generated by CO<sub>2</sub>-etching. Comprehensive in situ and ex situ tests unravel that the high Na<sup>+</sup> storage performance originates from the pore-filling mechanism in the closed micropores.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202303064-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202303064-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are being developed as a low-cost alternative to the dominating lithium-ion batteries, due to the abundant sodium resource, excellent low-temperature performance, and fast-charging capability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303064-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303064-bib-0001_R_d32609328e464" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Acc. Chem. Res.">1</a>-<a href="#aenm202303064-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303064-bib-0003_R_d32609328e464" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Nat. Commun.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> While several cathode materials (such as layered oxides,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303064-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303064-bib-0004_R_d32609328e469" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Science">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> polyanionic compounds,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303064-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303064-bib-0005_R_d32609328e474" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Small">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Prussian blues)<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303064-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303064-bib-0006_R_d32609328e480" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Battery Energy">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> have been well developed, the lack of high-performance anode materials with high Na-storage capacity and low cost is a major challenge for developing large-energy SIBs. Graphites are used as anode material in commercial lithium-ion batteries (LIBs). However, intercalation of Na<sup>+</sup> ions into graphite to form binary graphite intercalation graphite (b-GICs) are thermodynamically unfavorable.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303064-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303064-bib-0007_R_d32609328e487" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Mater.">7</a>, <a href="#aenm202303064-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303064-bib-0008_R_d32609328e487" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Energy Mater.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Until now, “hard carbon” as a kind of non-graphitizable carbon, has become the most suitable SIB anode owing to their randomly distributed turbostratic microstructure, large interlayer spacing (over 0.37&nbsp;nm), and closed nanopores.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303064-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303064-bib-0009_R_d32609328e493" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Funct. Mater. Lett.">9</a>, <a href="#aenm202303064-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303064-bib-0010_R_d32609328e493" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Adv. Energy Mater.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Generally, hard carbon is synthesized by high-temperature carbonization of the thermosetting precursors (e.g., cellulose, peanut shells, sucrose, glucose, phenolic resin, starch, anthracite).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303064-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303064-bib-0011_R_d32609328e500" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Carbon Energy">11</a>-<a href="#aenm202303064-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303064-bib-0014_R_d32609328e500" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Nano Energy">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> During pyrolysis, carbon layer in precursors are prone to forming turbostratic and disordered microstructures that are composed of surface defects, nanopores or voids, and graphitic domains. During the sodiation process, Na ions are first adsorbed on the defect sites of hard carbon in the sloping region (0.1–1&nbsp;V vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>), and then quasi-metallic Na clusters are formed in the plateau region (0–0.1&nbsp;V vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303064-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303064-bib-0015_R_d32609328e509" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Small Methods">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Extensive research on hard carbon can be summarized as the following: 1) understanding the charge-storage mechanism (such as intercalation,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303064-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303064-bib-0016_R_d32609328e515" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - ACS Energy Lett">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> pore-filling),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303064-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303064-bib-0017_R_d32609328e520" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Mater. Today">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> 2) enhancing Na<sup>+</sup> transfer kinetics by introducing heteroatoms (e.g., O, N, S, P),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303064-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303064-bib-0018_R_d32609328e528" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Adv. Energy Mater.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> porosity or defects,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303064-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303064-bib-0019_R_d32609328e533" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Nano-Micro Lett">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> 3) optimizing electrode–electrolyte interface (such as layer-by-layer &amp; pseudo-SEI,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303064-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303064-bib-0020_R_d32609328e538" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Adv. Mater.">20</a>, <a href="#aenm202303064-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303064-bib-0021_R_d32609328e538" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Adv. Sci.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> weak Na<sup>+</sup> solvation/desolvation electrolyte),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303064-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303064-bib-0022_R_d32609328e546" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and 4) increasing the reversible Na-storage capacity and initial Coulombic efficiency by regulating pore structure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303064-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303064-bib-0023_R_d32609328e551" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Adv. Energy Mater.">23</a>, <a href="#aenm202303064-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303064-bib-0024_R_d32609328e551" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Mater. Today Energy">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Despite these efforts, these hard carbon electrodes still show rather low plateau capacities at ≈0.1&nbsp;V (Na<sup>+</sup>/Na), and the plateau capacity plays a key role in determining the operating voltage and the energy density of the full batteries.</p>
         
         <p>It has been recently demonstrated that closed pores are mainly responsible for sodium storage at the low-voltage plateau.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303064-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303064-bib-0025_R_d32609328e561" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Adv. Energy Mater.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To date, numerous efforts have been dedicated to increase the number and size of the closed nanopores to maximize the plateau capacity of the resultant hard carbon. For example, Titirici et al demonstrated that the capacity in the plateau region is proportional to the diameter of closed pores, and the sample carbonized at 1500&nbsp;°C shows the highest plateau capacity (216&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303064-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303064-bib-0026_R_d32609328e568" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Similar result is also demonstrated in Xia's work, where carbon spheres from phenolic resin exhibits a high plateau capacity of 248.2&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at an optimal carbonization temperature of 1900&nbsp;°C.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303064-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303064-bib-0027_R_d32609328e576" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - J. Power Sources">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Very recently, Hu et al reported a resin-derived hard carbon using ethanol as a pore-forming agent followed by carbonization at 1550&nbsp;°C, which could provide a high Na storage capacity of ca. 410&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, corresponding to the plateau capacity of 284&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303064-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303064-bib-0028_R_d32609328e586" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - ACS Energy Lett">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Despite these progress, the reported plateau capacities in most hard carbons (200–300&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) are still insufficient, and all lower than that of graphite (372&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) in LIBs.</p>
         
         <p>In order to generate more closed pores in hard carbon, some chemical-activation or template agents are usually added into carbon precursor. For instance, MnCl<sub>2</sub>-assisted catalytic carbonization can enhance the graphitization degree and eliminate some defects of tissue-derived hard carbon and its plateau capacity is increased from ≈215 to ≈246&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303064-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303064-bib-0029_R_d32609328e602" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Adv. Energy Mater.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, ZnO,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303064-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303064-bib-0030_R_d32609328e607" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Adv. Mater.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> MgO,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303064-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303064-bib-0031_R_d32609328e613" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> K<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303064-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303064-bib-0032_R_d32609328e623" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - J. Alloys Compd.">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> KOH,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303064-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303064-bib-0033_R_d32609328e628" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> ZnAc<sub>2</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303064-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303064-bib-0034_R_d32609328e635" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Adv. Mater.">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> etc. were also employed as chemical etching reagents to increase the volume of closed pores in hard carbons. Although the low-potential plateau capacity can be effectively increased in these reported hard carbons, these etching reagents add cost and are mostly corrosive, and worse, removing them will involve complicated and time-consuming acid dissolution, which inevitably results in environmental pollution, high energy consumption, and complexity of the production process. In particular, template removal will induce drastic structural collapse as well as intrinsic defects, leading to the formation of low-tap-density and low-ICE hard carbons. Hence, more cost effective and environmental friendly strategies to generating abundant closed pores in hard carbon is highly desirable.</p>
         
         <p>In this work, by using starch as a cheap and industry-viable precursor, we have developed a mild CO<sub>2</sub>-etching and carbonization strategy that is capable of induce a large quantity of closed nanopores. In the entire synthesis process, the only operation is to change the atmosphere from CO<sub>2</sub> to Ar. This facile and straightforward process is more suitable to scalable production. After CO<sub>2</sub> etching, the diameter of closed pores in starch-derived microspheres is enlarged from 3.82 to 4.86&nbsp;nm, and the corresponding pore volume is increased from 0.045 to 0.117&nbsp;cm<sup>3</sup>&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. As a result, the as-synthesized hard carbon electrode exhibits an extremely high reversible capacity (487.6&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 25&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) with a high ICE of 90.56%. The corresponding low-potential plateau capacity is as high as 351&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, compared to that of starch-derived hard carbon without CO<sub>2</sub>-etching (197&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>). The pore-filling mechanism for Na-ion storage is verified based on various electrochemical and in situ characterizations. Compared to the complicated chemical activation or template methods, our CO<sub>2</sub>-etching approach is facile and practically viable to promoting the commercialization of high-energy-density SIBs.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202303064-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202303064-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202303064-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="aenm202303064-sec-0030-title">2.1 Fabrication of Hard Carbon and Porosity Examination</h3>
            
            <p>The CO<sub>2</sub>-assisted synthesis process of starch-derived hard carbon microspheres (denoted as “HCMP-CO<sub>2</sub>”) contains mainly three steps (see schematics in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303064-fig-0001">1a</a>). First, starch is selected as the precursor of hard carbon due to its abundant resource and low cost as well as high carbon content.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303064-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303064-bib-0035_R_d32609328e692" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - J. Energy Chem.">35</a>, <a href="#aenm202303064-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303064-bib-0036_R_d32609328e692" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - J. Energy Chem.">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> More importantly, starch has a natural spherical morphology, which is desirable for battery application. However, pure starch is prone to melting and foaming during carbonization,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303064-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303064-bib-0037_R_d32609328e698" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - ACS Sustainable Chem. Eng.">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> which will destroy the microsphere skeleton and lead to a high surface area and low ICE. To eliminate the foaming phenomenon, esterified starch was synthesized by mixing starch and maleic anhydride in a reactor at 80&nbsp;°C. Fourier transformed infrared (FTIR) and X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) analysis (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1a</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2a,b</a>, Supporting Information) verify this esterification reaction. Noted that the original spherical shape (10–30&nbsp;µm in diameter) from starch is well maintained, and the carbon yield from esterified starch to hard carbon is ≈21% (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1b</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3a–d</a>, Supporting Information). When esterified starch is carbonized at high temperatures (ca. 1300&nbsp;°C), the obtained sample (denoted as “HCMP”) can still maintain the spherical shape (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3e,f</a>, Supporting Information). In contrast, direct carbonization of pure starch will form a 3D foamed carbon network with irregular fragments (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information), which result in a low tap density and carbon yield (less 10%). This result underlines the importance of ester grafting in maintaining the original microsphere morphology. In the second step, CO<sub>2</sub> gas is used to etch esterified starch at ca. 800&nbsp;°C, where abundant open micropores are created in the activated carbon matrix through the reaction (CO<sub>2</sub> + C → 2CO). <i>Finally</i>, a high-temperature carbonization process by replacing CO<sub>2</sub> with Ar promotes the rearrangement of carbon layers, leading to gradual closure of the open-porous structure and finally, we obtain hard carbon containing abundant closed pores.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202303064-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/437c0f8c-b5df-47ac-a813-b40afc01d126/aenm202303064-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/437c0f8c-b5df-47ac-a813-b40afc01d126/aenm202303064-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/9e13720b-f460-48ad-81be-a21ece4ba850/aenm202303064-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/437c0f8c-b5df-47ac-a813-b40afc01d126/aenm202303064-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202303064-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202303064" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Fabrication and structure of hard carbon with closed-pores microspheres. a) Schematic of the synthesis of HCMP-CO<sub>2</sub>. b) TEM images of CO<sub>2</sub>-etched carbon precursor (denoted as ES-CO<sub>2</sub>), c) SEM and d) TEM images of HCMP-CO<sub>2</sub>. e) XRD patterns, f) Comparison of <i>L</i><sub>a</sub> and <i>L</i><sub>c</sub>. g) Raman spectra of HCMP-CO<sub>2</sub> and HCMP.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The CO<sub>2</sub>-etched carbon precursor (denoted as “ES-CO<sub>2</sub>”) still retains the microsphere morphology after the thermal treatment (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303064-fig-0001">1b</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information), but exhibits numerous open nanopores. After annealing ES-CO<sub>2</sub> at 1300&nbsp;°C under an Ar atmosphere, the obtained HCMP-CO<sub>2</sub> sample is composed of typical microspheres with the diameter of 10–30&nbsp;µm (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303064-fig-0001">1c</a>). A large number of closed pores are observed that are surrounded by randomly and closely stacked carbon layers (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303064-fig-0001">1d</a>), with an interlayer spacing of 0.398&nbsp;nm. In contrast, the HCMP sample without CO<sub>2</sub>-etching also shows a typical structure of hard carbon, but has less closed pores and smaller interlayer distance (0.378&nbsp;nm) (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information). XRD patterns (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303064-fig-0001">1e</a>) indicate that HCMP-CO<sub>2</sub> exhibits a larger interlayer spacing of 0.396&nbsp;nm than that of HCMP (0.378&nbsp;nm), which is consistent with the TEM results. In addition, the microcrystalline structure of sample is derived from the stacking thickness <i>L</i><sub>c</sub> and lateral width <i>L</i><sub>a</sub> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303064-fig-0001">1f</a>), in which the <i>L</i><sub>a</sub> and <i>L</i><sub>c</sub> values reveal an incremental tendency from HCMP to HCMP-CO<sub>2</sub>, demonstrating the regulation of carbon microstructure by CO<sub>2</sub>-Etching. The area ratio of the typical D and G Raman peaks (<i>A</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>A</i><sub>G</sub>) increases from HCMP (1.83) to HCMP-CO<sub>2</sub> (2.15), indicating that CO<sub>2</sub> activation has induced more disorder or defects in accordance with the random alignment of carbon layers during high-temperature carbonization (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303064-fig-0001">1g</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <p>The chemical composition of HCMP and HCMP-CO<sub>2</sub> samples was investigated by XPS. XPS spectra show that both samples are mainly composed of carbon and oxygen elements (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8a,b</a>, Supporting Information). The oxygen content of HCMP-CO<sub>2</sub> (2.96&nbsp;wt.%) is slightly higher than that of HCMP (2.74&nbsp;wt.%). The C 1 s spectra can be fitted into three peaks with binding energies of 284.8, 286.3, and 289.4&nbsp;eV, which correspond to the C─C/C═C, C─O, and C═O, respectively. Among them, the C─O content is relatively higher for HCMP-CO<sub>2</sub> (30.9%) than for HCMP (25.6%), implying that CO<sub>2</sub>-etching can promote formation of epoxy bonds. The O 1 s spectra indicate that the oxygen element exists in three types of C═O, C─O, and ─OH groups. In comparison, HCMP-CO<sub>2</sub> has higher C═O and ─OH ratios than that of HCMP, which will contribute additional sloping capacity (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8c–f</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <p>To investigate the effect of CO<sub>2</sub>-etching on the surface area and pore structure of hard carbon, N<sub>2</sub> adsorption–desorption measurements are first performed. The N<sub>2</sub> adsorption–desorption isotherm of ES-CO<sub>2</sub> exhibits a type-I isotherm, indicating presence of abundant micropores (&gt;1&nbsp;nm). Based on the BET model, the surface area and pore volume of ES-CO<sub>2</sub> are measured to be 539.38&nbsp;m<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> and 0.28&nbsp;cm<sup>3</sup>&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303064-fig-0002">2a</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting Information). After high-temperature carbonization at 1300&nbsp;°C, the corresponding values of HCMP-CO<sub>2</sub> are significantly reduced to 2.29&nbsp;m<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> and 0.0004&nbsp;cm<sup>3</sup>&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. This result indicates that the as-formed open pores during the CO<sub>2</sub>-etching process are completely converted into closed pores, which are not accessed by N<sub>2</sub> molecules.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303064-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303064-bib-0038_R_d32609328e907" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Adv. Energy Mater.">38</a>, <a href="#aenm202303064-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303064-bib-0039_R_d32609328e907" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Mater. Today Energy">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In comparison, HCMP without CO<sub>2</sub>-etching exhibits a relatively higher surface area (30.32&nbsp;m<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) and larger pore volume (0.016&nbsp;cm<sup>3</sup>&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) than that of HCMP-CO<sub>2</sub>, demonstrating that direct carbonization can still retain a few open pores in the carbon surface. To investigate ultra-micropores in a width of &lt;1&nbsp;nm in both samples (HCMP-CO<sub>2</sub> and HCMP), CO<sub>2</sub> adsorption–desorption was further measured (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303064-fig-0002">2b</a>). As displayed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303064-fig-0002">2c</a>, the ultra-micropore size of HCMP-CO<sub>2</sub> is mainly distributed in the range of 0.4–0.8&nbsp;nm, and the corresponding pore volume is as high as 0.11&nbsp;cm<sup>3</sup>&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. These ultra-micropores have been demonstrated to provide transport channels for Na<sup>+</sup> insertion to enhance the plateau capacity, and also to inhibit the contact of the electrolyte with the inner carbon surfaces, thus resulting in a high ICE.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303064-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303064-bib-0040_R_d32609328e945" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">40</a>, <a href="#aenm202303064-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303064-bib-0041_R_d32609328e945" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - ACS Energy Lett">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In contrast, the HCMP sample obtained from direct carbonization exhibits a negligible CO<sub>2</sub>-adsorption amount with a significantly small pore volume of 0.007&nbsp;cm<sup>3</sup>&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202303064-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/5d1b574d-682b-41ab-b9dd-11a37780f6ab/aenm202303064-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/5d1b574d-682b-41ab-b9dd-11a37780f6ab/aenm202303064-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/7b1b9b5d-400c-4828-b66e-26df8e192ee2/aenm202303064-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/5d1b574d-682b-41ab-b9dd-11a37780f6ab/aenm202303064-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202303064-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202303064" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Porosity examination. a) N<sub>2</sub> adsorption–desorption isotherms of ES-CO<sub>2</sub>, HCMP and HCMP-CO<sub>2</sub>. b) CO<sub>2</sub> adsorption–desorption isotherms of HCMP and HCMP-CO<sub>2</sub>, Inset is the enlarged isotherm of HCMP. c) Pore size distribution of HCMP and HCMP-CO<sub>2</sub>. d) Small-angle X-ray scattering patterns. e) Closed pore diameter and closed pore volume. f) Schematic illustration of the pore structure evolution.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Further detailed structure of closed pores in HCMP and HCMP-CO<sub>2</sub> is elucidated by the small-angle X-ray scattering (SAXS) technique and the true density test based on Archimedes principle. SAXS pattern of HCMP-CO<sub>2</sub> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303064-fig-0002">2d</a>) shows an increase in scattering intensities at 0.1–0.2&nbsp;Å<sup>−1</sup> as compared to HCMP, indicating enlargement of closed pores. By fitting SAXS, we can obtain the detailed parameters of closed pores (details in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a>, Supporting Information). Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303064-fig-0002">2e</a> shows that the average size of closed pores in HCMP-CO<sub>2</sub> is ≈4.86&nbsp;nm, which is larger than that of HCMP (3.82&nbsp;nm), indicating that CO<sub>2</sub> etching is more conducive to increasing the size of closed pores. In the true density (<i>ρ</i><sub>true</sub>) test, helium gas was used to obtain the density of carbon skeleton as helium can enter all open pores at room temperature.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303064-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303064-bib-0042_R_d32609328e1019" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">42</a>, <a href="#aenm202303064-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303064-bib-0043_R_d32609328e1019" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Adv. Energy Mater.">43</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Given the fact that graphite with an ideal layered structure has no closed pores with a true density of 2.26&nbsp;g&nbsp;cm<sup>−3</sup>, the closed pore volume of hard carbon can be obtained by <i>V</i><sub>closed pore</sub> = 1/<i>ρ</i><sub>true</sub>−1/2.26.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303064-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303064-bib-0044_R_d32609328e1034" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - Adv. Energy Mater.">44</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As shown in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information), the closed pore volume (0.117&nbsp;cm<sup>3</sup>&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) of HCMP-CO<sub>2</sub> is ≈2.6 times that of HCMP (0.045&nbsp;cm<sup>3</sup>&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>), further corroborating that the open pores generated from CO<sub>2</sub> activation are almost fully closed during high-temperature carbonization (1300&nbsp;°C). The schematic in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303064-fig-0002">2f</a> illustrates the difference in pore structure and size in HCMP-CO<sub>2</sub> and HCMP.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202303064-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="aenm202303064-sec-0040-title">2.2 Comparison of Sodium-Ion Storage Property</h3>
            
            <p>It is important to elaborate the relationship between the closed pore structure and sodium-storage capacity. The galvanostatic charge–discharge (GCD) curves of HCMP and HCMP-CO<sub>2</sub> at a small current of 25&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303064-fig-0003">3a,b</a>) show two obvious regions, namely, the sloping region (above 0.1&nbsp;V) and the plateau region (below 0.1&nbsp;V). The reversible capacity and initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE) of HCMP-CO<sub>2</sub> is 487.6&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> and 90.56%, which are far higher than that of HCMP (302.2&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> and 88.43%). The increased ICE for HCMP-CO<sub>2</sub> is mainly due to its significantly low surface area (2.29&nbsp;m<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>). We further compare the sloping capacity and the plateau capacity of both samples (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303064-fig-0003">3c</a>). It is seen that the capacity increase in HCMP-CO<sub>2</sub> is mainly from the plateau capacity (351&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>), which corresponds to 72% of the total value. In contrast, the HCMP electrode exhibits a low percentage of plateau capacity (193.4&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, 64%). This result indicates that formation of abundant closed pores from CO<sub>2</sub>-etching plays an important role in the sodium storage performance of hard carbon. In addition to specific capacity and ICE, the rate capability of two anodes also show evident distinction (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303064-fig-0003">3d</a>). When the current density increases to 800&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, the Na-storage capacity of HCMP-CO<sub>2</sub> is measured to be 171&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, which is much higher than that of HCMP (36&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 800&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>). This sharp difference suggests the beneficial effect of CO<sub>2</sub>-induced closed pores in rate performance. After carefully comparing the difference in GCD curves at different current densities (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a> Supporting Information), it can be found that the capacity decay at high current densities is mainly from the loss of plateau capacity. This may be due to the large polarization of Na-deposition/stripping in closed pores at high rates, thus leading to the irreversible capacity loss.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303064-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303064-bib-0011_R_d32609328e1124" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Carbon Energy">11</a>, <a href="#aenm202303064-bib-0061" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303064-bib-0061_R_d32609328e1124" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 61 - Natl. Sci. Rev.">61</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Furthermore, the sodium storage performance of HCMP-CO<sub>2</sub> carbonized at 1300&nbsp;°C exceeds that of control samples obtained by carbonization at other temperatures (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a> Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202303064-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/6b4b3e36-8bca-4dba-ab34-08dd36062ce4/aenm202303064-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/6b4b3e36-8bca-4dba-ab34-08dd36062ce4/aenm202303064-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/ec3c3816-9f1d-4350-92d9-b28d2b1523d5/aenm202303064-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/6b4b3e36-8bca-4dba-ab34-08dd36062ce4/aenm202303064-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202303064-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202303064" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical properties of hard carbon anodes. Representative GCD profiles for a) HCMP and b) HCMP-CO<sub>2</sub> in the potential range of 0.01–3&nbsp;V at 25&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. c) Comparison of the plateau and sloping capacity of HCMP and HCMP-CO<sub>2</sub> based on the 2nd discharge/charge curves. d) Rate capability and e) cycling performance at 200&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> of HCMP-CO<sub>2</sub> and HCMP. f) Comparison of the plateau capacities and initial Coulombic efficiencies (denoted by both color and size of the circles) for HCMP-CO<sub>2</sub> in this work with hard carbons reported in literature.<sup>[</sup><span class="figureLink bibLink tab-link"><sup><a href="#aenm202303064-bib-0025" class="figureLink bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303064-bib-0025_R_d32609328e1167" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Adv. Energy Mater.">25</a>, <a href="#aenm202303064-bib-0026" class="figureLink bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303064-bib-0026_R_d32609328e1167" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">26</a>, <a href="#aenm202303064-bib-0028" class="figureLink bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303064-bib-0028_R_d32609328e1167" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - ACS Energy Lett">28</a>, <a href="#aenm202303064-bib-0029" class="figureLink bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303064-bib-0029_R_d32609328e1167" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Adv. Energy Mater.">29</a>, <a href="#aenm202303064-bib-0033" class="figureLink bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303064-bib-0033_R_d32609328e1167" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">33</a>, <a href="#aenm202303064-bib-0034" class="figureLink bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303064-bib-0034_R_d32609328e1167" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Adv. Mater.">34</a>, <a href="#aenm202303064-bib-0044" class="figureLink bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303064-bib-0044_R_d32609328e1167" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - Adv. Energy Mater.">44</a>-<a href="#aenm202303064-bib-0060" class="figureLink bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303064-bib-0060_R_d32609328e1167" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 60 - Carbon">60</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The cycling stability of HCMP and HCMP-CO<sub>2</sub> electrodes is further measured at a current of 200&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. HCMP-CO<sub>2</sub> shows 96% capacity retention after 100 cycles with the stable Coulomb efficiency of 100% (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303064-fig-0003">3e</a>). In contrast, the capacity loss is observed from the HCMP which drops from initial 238&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> to 131&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at the 100th cycle. This indicates that HCMP-CO<sub>2</sub> is structurally stable during the charge/discharge process. More importantly, Na-storage performance of our HCMP-CO<sub>2</sub> is superior to those of commercial hard carbon (such as type 2) (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12e</a> Supporting Information) and previously reported hard carbon electrodes in literature in terms of specific capacity and ICE (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303064-fig-0003">3f</a>; Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information). Particularly, the high plateau capacity (351&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) in HCMP-CO<sub>2</sub> is even close to that of the graphite anode (372&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) in LIBs. To our knowledge, this value is among the best for starch-derived hard carbons reported to date,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303064-bib-0062" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303064-bib-0062_R_d32609328e1208" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 62 - Carbon">62</a>-<a href="#aenm202303064-bib-0064" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303064-bib-0064_R_d32609328e1208" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 64 - Mater. Today Energy">64</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> demonstrating its promising potential for SIB commercialization.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202303064-sec-0050">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="aenm202303064-sec-0050-title">2.3 Charge-Storage Kinetics Analysis</h3>
            
            <p>To further understand the Na-storage mechanism of HCMP-CO<sub>2</sub> in the low voltage plateau region, we let quasi-metallic Na react with ethanol to form H<sub>2</sub> and CH<sub>3</sub>CH<sub>2</sub>ONa. The reaction will turn the phenolphthalein solution to be red. <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303064-fig-0004">4a</a> illustrates that the ethanol solution gradually appears color during the discharge process. When discharged to 0.01&nbsp;V, HCMP-CO<sub>2</sub> produces a large number of bubbles and the solution color changes to dark red, which indicates the presence of quasi-metallic sodium in HCMP-CO<sub>2</sub>, corresponding to Na filling in closed pores. Cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves of HCMP-CO<sub>2</sub> and HCMP samples (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13a-c</a>, Supporting Information) show two pairs of representative peaks. Moreover, a weak irreversible reduction peak at 0.4&nbsp;V in the first cycle corresponds to the decomposition of the electrolyte to form a SEI layer. In initial three cycles, all CV curves are nearly overlapped, indicating reversibility of electrochemical reactions. Compared to HCMP, the redox peak of HCMP-CO<sub>2</sub> is more obvious due to a larger plateau capacity. To further understand the sodium storage behavior, the CV curves at different scan rates were recorded to analyze the reaction kinetics (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303064-fig-0004">4b,c</a>). It can be seen that the peak current of HCMP-CO<sub>2</sub> increases with the scan rate more intensively than the HCMP electrode. Based on the relationship (<i>i</i> = a<i>v</i><sup>b</sup>)<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303064-bib-0065" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303064-bib-0065_R_d32609328e1259" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 65 - Energy Environ. Sci.">65</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> between the peak current (<i>i</i>) and scan rate (<i>v</i>) (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12b</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13d</a>, Supporting Information), the b values of peak (A, B) in HCMP-CO<sub>2</sub> are 0.53 and 0.88, which are higher than those of HCMP (0.43 and 0.61). The higher <i>b</i> value indicates faster reaction kinetics of HCMP-CO<sub>2</sub> than HCMP in terms of diffusion-controlled plateau capacity as well as surface-controlled slope capacity. Further, the CV curves are fitted by differentiating the currents contributed by surface-controlled process and diffusion-controlled reactions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303064-bib-0065" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303064-bib-0065_R_d32609328e1282" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 65 - Energy Environ. Sci.">65</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The fitting result (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303064-fig-0004">4d</a>) illustrates that the sloping region is mainly dominated by the surface-controlled currents, whereas the diffusion-controlled currents account for the plateau region. All in all, the CO<sub>2</sub> activation has generated not only more active sites to enhance the surface-controlled reactions, but also a large number of closed pores for sodium filling, leading to excellent Na-storage kinetics in the HCMP-CO<sub>2</sub> electrode.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202303064-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/0072795b-a373-40dd-99e8-b98c5687d692/aenm202303064-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/0072795b-a373-40dd-99e8-b98c5687d692/aenm202303064-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/74a76934-92ba-4600-9091-eeb1831f5db8/aenm202303064-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/0072795b-a373-40dd-99e8-b98c5687d692/aenm202303064-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202303064-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202303064" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Charge storage kinetics and mechanism. a) Typical charge–discharge curves of HCMP-CO<sub>2</sub> and the corresponding color change of ethanol containing 1% phenolphthalein after reaction with the HCMP-CO<sub>2</sub> electrode at different potentials. b,c) CV curves of HCMP-CO<sub>2</sub> and HCMP at different scan rates (all from 2nd cycle). d) Calculated contributions of the diffusion- and surface-controlled proportions varying from 0.2 to 1.0&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup> in HCMP-CO<sub>2</sub> and HCMP. e,f) Sodium-ion diffusion coefficients at sodiation and de-sodiation process estimated from GITT curves. g) Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) of HCMP-CO<sub>2</sub> and HCMP electrodes. Inset is the zoomed view of EIS curves of HCMP-CO<sub>2</sub>. h) Proposed sodiation process of HCMP-CO<sub>2</sub> at different discharge stages.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>In addition, the two electrodes were analysed by galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) at a pulse current of 25&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a>, Supporting Information). The diffusion coefficient (<i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub>) calculated from the sodiation and desodiation process are presented in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303064-fig-0004">4e,f</a>. The <i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub> value varied insignificantly in the slope region, and a high diffusivity of Na<sup>+</sup> corresponds to the adsorption on the carbon surface. In the plateau region, <i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub> drops sharply with a U-turn feature. The rapid decrease in <i>D</i> values at ≈ 0.1–0.05&nbsp;V is perhaps due to the intercalation of Na<sup>+</sup> through the carbon layer with the interior of HCMP-CO<sub>2</sub>, while the rebound in the diffusion coefficient at 0.05&nbsp;V is ascribed to sodium ions starting to fill the nanopores. Nonetheless, both in the slope and plateau region, HCMP-CO<sub>2</sub> exhibits a higher <i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub> than HCMP. Furthermore, according to electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) measurement (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303064-fig-0004">4g</a>), the HCMP-CO<sub>2</sub> electrode has a much smaller charge-transfer impedance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>) than that of HCMP both before cycling and after ten cycles. To conclude, the modulation of the closed pore structure is beneficial to achieving fast charge transfer and small polarization (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a>, Supporting Information), leading to high reaction kinetics for Na-ion transport and storage.</p>
            
            <p>The structural evolution of HCMP-CO<sub>2</sub> during charge and discharge has been investigated using in situ Raman spectroscopy, ex situ XRD, and ex situ XPS. As shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303064-fig-0005">5</a>, the D-band Raman peak gradually widens when the potential is changed from 1.4 to 0.1&nbsp;V during discharge, but the peak position remains unchanged, indicating that the sodium ions in the slope region are mainly controlled by adsorption. At the plateau region of 0.1–0.01&nbsp;V, the G-band shifts to a lower frequency of 1500&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup>. This could be because electrons are located on the <i>Π</i><sup>*</sup> band of carbon layer,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202303064-bib-0066" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202303064-bib-0066_R_d32609328e1395" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 66 - Adv. Mater.">66</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> thus inducing the electron–phonon coupling and the formation of Na─C interaction. Meanwhile, the D-band almost vanishes, indicating that the defects in carbon surface is covered by the Na clusters. Ex situ XRD patterns of HCMP-CO<sub>2</sub> at different voltages (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16a</a>, Supporting Information) show that during the discharging process from 1 to 0.1&nbsp;V, there is no shift in the (002) peak, indicating that Na<sup>+</sup> is mainly adsorbed on the carbon surface in the slope region. Further discharging to 0.01&nbsp;V causes a significant leftward shift of the (002) peak, corresponding to the sodium filling within the nanopores in the plateau region. This is supported by the peak shift of the quasi-metallic sodium in ex situ XPS (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16b</a>, Supporting Information). In the slope region, the peak from quasi-metallic sodium shifts with continuous discharging. After entering the plateau region, the peak position remains intact but the peak intensity of metallic sodium increases significantly, indicating that sodium begins to fill in the closed pores. All these results demonstrate that sodium ions primarily undergo adsorption in the slope region, intercalation into the carbon layers, and further reduction to sodium clusters filling in enclosed pores in the plateau region (see schematic illustration in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303064-fig-0004">4h</a>).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202303064-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/65a49348-5ad5-4b43-b26e-a1297a351bb6/aenm202303064-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/65a49348-5ad5-4b43-b26e-a1297a351bb6/aenm202303064-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/70ca2a9d-aed6-4b38-a76c-ee08ed2d6efb/aenm202303064-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/65a49348-5ad5-4b43-b26e-a1297a351bb6/aenm202303064-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202303064-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202303064" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">In situ Raman characterization. a) In situ Raman spectra with time-potential of the HCMP-CO<sub>2</sub> electrode during the first charge/discharge cycle. b,c) In situ Raman spectroscopy of HCMP-CO<sub>2</sub> during the discharge/charge process.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>To demonstrate the versatility of our CO<sub>2</sub>-assisted pore-regulation strategy to other types of hard carbon, polyacrylonitrile (PAN) was used as the raw material to undergo the same process, i.e., CO<sub>2</sub> activation at 800&nbsp;°C and subsequent high-temperature carbonization at 1300&nbsp;°C in the Ar atmosphere. As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17</a> (Supporting Information), the sodium-storage capacity of the as-obtained hard carbon increases to 332&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> as compared to the PAN after a direct carbonization at 1300&nbsp;°C without CO<sub>2</sub>-etching (231&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>). The capacity increase originates mainly from the plateau region, which agrees with HCMP-CO<sub>2</sub>. Hence, it is believed that our CO<sub>2</sub>-etching strategy in this work could have a certain universality to enhancing the Na-storage property of other biomass (or resin)-based hard carbons. In another control experiment, we employed KOH to etch esterified starch (denoted as “ES-KOH”). The surface area of ES-KOH (1788.95&nbsp;m<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) is much higher than that of ES-CO<sub>2</sub> (539.38&nbsp;m<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>). However, after the carbonization at 1300&nbsp;°C, the obtained sample (referred as HCMP-KOH) shows no obvious closed pores from the HRTEM observation (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18</a> in Supporting Information). Possible reason for this is that the KOH activation leads to an excessive formation of open pores, which will destroy the rearrangement of carbon layers during high-temperature carbonization. As expected, the HCMP-KOH electrode displays no obvious plateau capacity, corresponding to a rather low sodium-storage capacity of only 115&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. This result corroborates the effectiveness of pore-regulation strategy in improving the plateau capacity of hard carbons.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202303064-sec-0060">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="aenm202303064-sec-0060-title">2.4 HCMP-CO<sub>2</sub>||NVP Full SIB Device</h3>
            
            <p>Finally, full cell battery is constructed by deploying HCMP-CO<sub>2</sub> anode and the Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NVP) cathode (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303064-fig-0006">6a</a>). The structural information and electrochemical property of the synthesized NVP is displayed in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19 and S20</a> (Supporting Information). Based on the specific capacities of HCMP-CO<sub>2</sub> and NVP, a mass ratio of <i>m</i><sub>HCMP-CO2</sub>:<i>m</i><sub>NVP</sub> = 1:3 is used to ensure the overall performance of the full cell. The as-assembled SIB exhibits an average working voltage of 3.31&nbsp;V (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303064-fig-0006">6b</a>). Even when the current density goes up to 600&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, the charge–discharge plateau can be well retained, demonstrating a superior rate capability, with a high reversible capacity of 61&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 600&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303064-fig-0006">6c</a>). Moreover, a capacity retention of 80.9% after 800 cycles is achieved (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202303064-fig-0006">6d</a>). The SIB full cell can provide an energy density of 281&nbsp;Wh&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup> at a small power density of 74&nbsp;W&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup> (based on the total mass of both anode and cathode materials). At a large power of 1800&nbsp;W&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup>, the energy density is as high as 181&nbsp;Wh&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup>. Furthermore, the full cells have also been tested at different temperatures from −20 to 60&nbsp;°C (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21</a>, Supporting Information). In general, the high-temperature performance exceeds that at −10&nbsp;°C and −20&nbsp;°C. Electrolyte additive can be considered to improve the low-temperature performance.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202303064-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/cbd19fb1-a717-490b-a98b-55e4287c570c/aenm202303064-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/cbd19fb1-a717-490b-a98b-55e4287c570c/aenm202303064-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/8d7b66a4-df58-493e-88c2-888fd95e773e/aenm202303064-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/cbd19fb1-a717-490b-a98b-55e4287c570c/aenm202303064-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202303064-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202303064" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Full SIB devices. a) The schematic illustration of the full configuration, where the HCMP-CO<sub>2</sub> anode is coupled with the Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cathode during the charge process. b) Charge/discharge curves of HCMP-CO<sub>2</sub>//NVP SIBs at various current densities. c) The capacity and Coulombic efficiency measured at various current densities. d) Cycling performance of HCMP-CO<sub>2</sub>//NVP SIB at a current density of 200&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
         </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202303064-sec-0070">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202303064-sec-0070-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>We have successfully developed a facile and scalable strategy for the synthesis of starch-derived hard carbon through CO<sub>2</sub> activation followed by high-temperature carbonization for sodium-ion storage. The CO<sub>2</sub> etching at a relatively low temperature (ca. 800&nbsp;°C) creates a large number of open pores, which are converted to closed pores after the subsequent carbonization at high temperature (ca. 1300&nbsp;°C). The obtained hard carbon contains microspheres with the size of 10–30&nbsp;µm. These closed pores in the microspheres render the HCMP-CO<sub>2</sub> anode with an ultrahigh specific capacity of 487.6&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> with an ICE of 90.56% for Na ion storage. The corresponding plateau capacity (351&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) in HCMP-CO<sub>2</sub> is much higher than that of starch-derived hard carbon without CO<sub>2</sub>-etching (193&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>), and even close to that of the graphite anode (372&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) in lithium-ion batteries. The high Na-storage performance in this new type of hard carbon is ascribed to the Na-filling within closed pores. The fabricated HCMP-CO<sub>2</sub>//NVP full cell exhibits a high energy density of 281&nbsp;Wh&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup>. Hopefully our proposed CO<sub>2</sub>-assisted pore-regulation strategy can be extended to other biomass (or resin)-based hard carbons, which will promote high sodium-storage plateau capacities and push the development of high-energy practical SIBs.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
